<li><a href="/wiki/Norway_Corporation" title="Norway Corporation">Norway Corporation</a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Budget</th><td class="infobox-data"><i>Per episode:</i><br />Season 1: $190,000<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>a<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><br />Season 2: $185,000<br />Season 3: $175,000</td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header summary">Original release</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Network</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Release</th><td class="infobox-data">September 8, 1966<span style="display: none;"> (<span class="bday dtstart published updated itvstart">1966-09-08</span>)</span>&#160;–<br />June 3, 1969<span style="display: none;"> (<span class="dtend itvend">1969-06-03</span>)</span></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header summary">Related</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409" /><div class="plainlist">
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_The_Animated_Series" title="Star Trek: The Animated Series">Star Trek: The Animated Series</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek#Television" title="Star Trek"><i>Star Trek</i> TV series</a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><i><b>Star Trek</b></i> is an American <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction_on_television" title="Science fiction on television">science fiction television series</a> created by <a href="/wiki/Gene_Roddenberry" title="Gene Roddenberry">Gene Roddenberry</a> that follows the adventures of the starship <a href="/wiki/USS_Enterprise_(NCC-1701)" title="USS Enterprise (NCC-1701)">USS&#160;<i>Enterprise</i>&#160;(NCC-1701)</a> and its crew. It acquired the <a href="/wiki/Retronym" title="Retronym">retronym</a> of <i><b>Star Trek: The Original Series</b></i> (<i><b>TOS</b></i><span style="padding-left:0.15em;">&#41;</span> to distinguish the show within the <a href="/wiki/Star_Trek" title="Star Trek">media franchise that it began</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-tos_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tos-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The show is set in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way galaxy</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 2266–2269. The ship and crew are led by Captain <a href="/wiki/James_T._Kirk" title="James T. Kirk">James T. Kirk</a> (<a href="/wiki/William_Shatner" title="William Shatner">William Shatner</a>), First Officer and Science Officer <a href="/wiki/Spock" title="Spock">Spock</a> (<a href="/wiki/Leonard_Nimoy" title="Leonard Nimoy">Leonard Nimoy</a>) and Chief Medical Officer <a href="/wiki/Leonard_McCoy" title="Leonard McCoy">Leonard H. "Bones" McCoy</a> (<a href="/wiki/DeForest_Kelley" title="DeForest Kelley">DeForest Kelley</a>). Shatner's voice-over introduction during each episode's opening credits stated the starship's purpose:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Space: the final frontier. These are the voyages of the starship <i>Enterprise</i>. Its five-year mission: to explore strange new worlds, to seek out new life and new civilizations, to boldly go where no man has gone before.</p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Norway_Productions" class="mw-redirect" title="Norway Productions">Norway Productions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Desilu_Productions" class="mw-redirect" title="Desilu Productions">Desilu Productions</a> produced the series from September 1966 to December 1967. <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Television_(original)" class="mw-redirect" title="Paramount Television (original)">Paramount Television</a> produced the show from January 1968 to June 1969. <i>Star Trek</i> aired on <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a> from September 8, 1966, to June 3, 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It was first broadcast on September 6, 1966, on Canada's <a href="/wiki/CTV_Television_Network" title="CTV Television Network">CTV</a> network.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While on NBC, <i>Star Trek</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s <a href="/wiki/Nielsen_ratings" class="mw-redirect" title="Nielsen ratings">Nielsen ratings</a> were low and the network canceled it after three seasons and 79 episodes. In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> the series was not broadcast until July 12, 1969, coinciding with the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a> mission to land the first humans on the Moon.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Through <a href="/wiki/Broadcast_syndication" title="Broadcast syndication">broadcast syndication</a> it became an international success in the 1970s, achieving <a href="/wiki/Cult_following" title="Cult following">cult classic</a> status and a developing <a href="/wiki/Cultural_influence_of_Star_Trek" title="Cultural influence of Star Trek">influence on popular culture</a>. <i>Star Trek</i> eventually spawned a <a href="/wiki/Media_franchise" title="Media franchise">media franchise</a> consisting of 11 television series, 13 feature films, and numerous books, games, and toys, and is now widely considered one of the most popular and influential television series of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-Asherman_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asherman-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Creation">Creation</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Creation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>On March 11, 1964, <a href="/wiki/Gene_Roddenberry" title="Gene Roddenberry">Gene Roddenberry</a>, a long-time fan of <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a>, drafted a short <a href="/wiki/Film_treatment" title="Film treatment">treatment</a> for a science-fiction television series that he called <i>Star Trek</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-STPitch1_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-STPitch1-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This was to be set on board a large <a href="/wiki/Starship" title="Starship">starship</a> named S.S. <i>Yorktown</i> in the 23rd century<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> bearing a crew dedicated to exploring the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxy</a>.
</p><p>Roddenberry noted a number of influences on his idea, some of which includes <a href="/wiki/A._E._van_Vogt" title="A. E. van Vogt">A. E. van Vogt</a>'s tales of the spaceship <i><a href="/wiki/The_Voyage_of_the_Space_Beagle" title="The Voyage of the Space Beagle">Space Beagle</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Eric_Frank_Russell" title="Eric Frank Russell">Eric Frank Russell</a>'s <i>Marathon</i> series of stories, and the film <i><a href="/wiki/Forbidden_Planet" title="Forbidden Planet">Forbidden Planet</a></i> (1956). Some have also drawn parallels with the television series <i><a href="/wiki/Rocky_Jones,_Space_Ranger" title="Rocky Jones, Space Ranger">Rocky Jones, Space Ranger</a></i> (1954), a <a href="/wiki/Space_opera" title="Space opera">space opera</a> that included many of the elements integral to <i>Star Trek</i>—the organization, crew relationships, missions, part of the bridge layout, and some technology.<sup id="cite_ref-Asherman_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asherman-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 24">&#58;&#8202;24&#8202;</span></sup> Roddenberry also drew heavily from <a href="/wiki/C._S._Forester" title="C. S. Forester">C. S. Forester</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Horatio_Hornblower" title="Horatio Hornblower">Horatio Hornblower</a> novels that depict a daring sea captain who exercises broad discretionary authority on distant sea missions of noble purpose. He often humorously referred to Captain Kirk as "Horatio Hornblower in Space".<sup id="cite_ref-whitfield1968_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whitfield1968-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Roddenberry had extensive experience in writing for <a href="/wiki/Western_(genre)" title="Western (genre)">series about the Old West</a> that had been popular television fare in the 1950s and 1960s. Armed with this background, he characterized the new show in his first draft as "<i><a href="/wiki/Wagon_Train" title="Wagon Train">Wagon Train</a></i> to the stars".<sup id="cite_ref-STPitch1_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-STPitch1-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25th_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25th-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Like the familiar <i>Wagon Train</i>, each episode was to be a self-contained adventure story, set within the structure of a continuing voyage through space.
</p><p>In Roddenberry's original concept, the protagonist was Captain <a href="/wiki/Robert_April" title="Robert April">Robert April</a> of the starship S.S. <i>Yorktown</i>. This character was developed into <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Pike_(Star_Trek)" title="Christopher Pike (Star Trek)">Captain Christopher Pike</a>, first portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Hunter" title="Jeffrey Hunter">Jeffrey Hunter</a>. April is listed in the <i>Star Trek Chronology</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Star_Trek_Encyclopedia" title="The Star Trek Encyclopedia">The Star Trek Encyclopedia</a></i>, and at startrek.com as the <i>Enterprise</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s first commanding officer, preceding Captain Pike.<sup id="cite_ref-Chrono_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chrono-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Encyc_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyc-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The character's only television/movie appearance was in the <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_The_Animated_Series" title="Star Trek: The Animated Series">Star Trek: The Animated Series</a></i> episode "<a href="/wiki/The_Counter-Clock_Incident" title="The Counter-Clock Incident">The Counter-Clock Incident</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-CCI_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CCI-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> until <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_Strange_New_Worlds" title="Star Trek: Strange New Worlds">Star Trek: Strange New Worlds</a></i>, where he is portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Holmes" title="Adrian Holmes">Adrian Holmes</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Bridges" title="Lloyd Bridges">Lloyd Bridges</a> was offered a starring role on what became <i>Star Trek</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bridges declined, saying he got along well with Roddenberry on a personal level but had no desire to work in science fiction.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Development">Development</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Development"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In April 1964, Roddenberry presented the <i>Star Trek</i> draft to Desilu Productions, a leading independent television production company.<sup id="cite_ref-startrekhistory.com_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-startrekhistory.com-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He met with <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Franklin_Solow" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbert Franklin Solow">Herbert F. Solow</a>, Desilu's director of production. Solow saw promise in the idea and signed a three-year program-development contract with Roddenberry.<sup id="cite_ref-dartmouth.org_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dartmouth.org-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lucille_Ball" title="Lucille Ball">Lucille Ball</a>, head of Desilu, was not familiar with the nature of the project, but she was instrumental in getting the pilot produced.<sup id="cite_ref-buinessinsider_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buinessinsider-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The concept was extensively revised and fleshed out during this time—"<a href="/wiki/The_Cage_(Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series)" title="The Cage (Star Trek: The Original Series)">The Cage</a>" pilot filmed in late 1964 differs in many respects from the March 1964 treatment. Solow, for example, added the "<a href="/wiki/Stardate" title="Stardate">stardate</a>" concept.<sup id="cite_ref-dartmouth.org_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dartmouth.org-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Desilu Productions had a <a href="/wiki/First-look_deal" title="First-look deal">first-look deal</a> with <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-memory-alpha.org_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-memory-alpha.org-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Oscar Katz, Desilu's Vice President of Production, went with Roddenberry to pitch the series to the network.<sup id="cite_ref-memory-alpha.org1_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-memory-alpha.org1-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They refused to purchase the show, as they already had a similar program in development, the 1965 <a href="/wiki/Irwin_Allen" title="Irwin Allen">Irwin Allen</a> series <i><a href="/wiki/Lost_in_Space" title="Lost in Space">Lost in Space</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-PearsonDavies2014_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PearsonDavies2014-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In May 1964, Solow, who had previously worked at <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a>, met with <a href="/wiki/Grant_Tinker" title="Grant Tinker">Grant Tinker</a>, then head of the network's West Coast programming department. Tinker commissioned the first pilot—which became "The Cage".<sup id="cite_ref-startrekhistory.com_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-startrekhistory.com-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> NBC turned down the resulting pilot, stating that it was "too cerebral".<sup id="cite_ref-davies2007_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies2007-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, the NBC executives were still impressed with the concept, and they understood that its perceived faults had been partly due to the script that they had selected themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-whitfield1968_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whitfield1968-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>NBC made the unusual decision to pay for a second pilot, using the script called "<a href="/wiki/Where_No_Man_Has_Gone_Before" title="Where No Man Has Gone Before">Where No Man Has Gone Before</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-davies2007_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies2007-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Only the character of <a href="/wiki/Spock" title="Spock">Spock</a>, played by <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Nimoy" title="Leonard Nimoy">Leonard Nimoy</a>, was retained from the first pilot, and only two cast members, <a href="/wiki/Majel_Barrett" title="Majel Barrett">Majel Barrett</a> and Nimoy, were carried forward into the series. This second pilot proved to be satisfactory to NBC, and the network selected <i>Star Trek</i> to be in its upcoming television schedule for the fall of 1966.
</p><p>The second pilot introduced most of the other main <a href="/wiki/List_of_Star_Trek_characters" title="List of Star Trek characters">characters</a>: Captain Kirk (<a href="/wiki/William_Shatner" title="William Shatner">William Shatner</a>), Chief Engineer Lt. Commander <a href="/wiki/Montgomery_Scott" class="mw-redirect" title="Montgomery Scott">Scott</a> (<a href="/wiki/James_Doohan" title="James Doohan">James Doohan</a>) and Lt. <a href="/wiki/Hikaru_Sulu" title="Hikaru Sulu">Sulu</a> (<a href="/wiki/George_Takei" title="George Takei">George Takei</a>), who served as a physicist on the ship in the second pilot, but subsequently became a helmsman throughout the rest of the series. <a href="/wiki/Paul_Fix" title="Paul Fix">Paul Fix</a> played Dr. Mark Piper in the second pilot; ship's doctor <a href="/wiki/Leonard_McCoy" title="Leonard McCoy">Leonard McCoy</a> (<a href="/wiki/DeForest_Kelley" title="DeForest Kelley">DeForest Kelley</a>) joined the cast when filming began for the first season, and he remained for the rest of the series, achieving billing as the third star of the series. Also joining the ship's permanent crew during the first season were the communications officer, Lt. <a href="/wiki/Nyota_Uhura" title="Nyota Uhura">Nyota Uhura</a> (<a href="/wiki/Nichelle_Nichols" title="Nichelle Nichols">Nichelle Nichols</a>), the first African-American woman to hold such an important role in an American television series;<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the captain's <a href="/wiki/Yeoman_(United_States_Navy)" title="Yeoman (United States Navy)">yeoman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Janice_Rand" title="Janice Rand">Janice Rand</a> (<a href="/wiki/Grace_Lee_Whitney" title="Grace Lee Whitney">Grace Lee Whitney</a>), who departed midway through the first season; and <a href="/wiki/Christine_Chapel" title="Christine Chapel">Christine Chapel</a> (Majel Barrett), the ship's nurse and assistant to McCoy. <a href="/wiki/Walter_Koenig" title="Walter Koenig">Walter Koenig</a> joined the cast as Ensign <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Chekov" title="Pavel Chekov">Pavel Chekov</a> in the series' second season.
</p><p>In February 1966, before the first episode was aired, <i>Star Trek</i> was nearly canceled by Desilu Productions. Desilu had gone from making just one half-hour show (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Lucy_Show" title="The Lucy Show">The Lucy Show</a></i>) to deficit-financing a portion of two expensive hour-long shows, <i><a href="/wiki/Mission:_Impossible_(1966_TV_series)" title="Mission: Impossible (1966 TV series)">Mission: Impossible</a></i> and <i>Star Trek</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Solow was able to convince Lucille Ball that both shows should continue.<sup id="cite_ref-memory-alpha.org_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-memory-alpha.org-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Production">Production</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Production"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:STIn_Beauty.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/29/STIn_Beauty.jpg/250px-STIn_Beauty.jpg" decoding="async" width="230" height="174" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/29/STIn_Beauty.jpg/345px-STIn_Beauty.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/29/STIn_Beauty.jpg 2x" data-file-width="363" data-file-height="274" /></a><figcaption>The original <a href="/wiki/Starship_Enterprise" title="Starship Enterprise">starship <i>Enterprise</i></a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Once the series had been picked up by NBC, the production moved to what was then Desilu Productions' Gower Street location. It had previously been the main studio complex used by <a href="/wiki/RKO_Pictures" title="RKO Pictures">RKO Pictures</a> and is now part of the <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures" title="Paramount Pictures">Paramount Pictures</a> lot. The series used what are now stages 31 and 32.<sup id="cite_ref-memory-alpha.org_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-memory-alpha.org-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The show's production staff included art director <a href="/wiki/Matt_Jefferies" title="Matt Jefferies">Matt Jefferies</a>, who designed the starship <i>Enterprise</i> and most of its interiors.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> His contributions to the series were honored in the name of the "<a href="/wiki/Jefferies_tube" class="mw-redirect" title="Jefferies tube">Jefferies tube</a>", an equipment shaft depicted in various <i>Star Trek</i> series. In addition to working with his brother, John Jefferies, to create the hand-held phaser weapons of <i>Star Trek</i>, Jefferies also developed the set design for the bridge of the <i>Enterprise</i> (which was based on an earlier design by <a href="/wiki/Pato_Guzman" title="Pato Guzman">Pato Guzman</a>). Jefferies used his practical experience as an airman during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> and his knowledge of aircraft design to devise a functional, and <a href="/wiki/Human_factors_and_ergonomics" class="mw-redirect" title="Human factors and ergonomics">ergonomic</a> bridge layout.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The costume designer for <i>Star Trek</i>, <a href="/wiki/William_Ware_Theiss" title="William Ware Theiss">Bill Theiss</a>, created the look of the <a href="/wiki/Star_Trek_uniforms" title="Star Trek uniforms">Starfleet uniforms</a> for the <i>Enterprise</i>, the costumes for female guest stars, and for various <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrials" class="mw-redirect" title="Extraterrestrials">aliens</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Klingon" title="Klingon">Klingons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vulcan_(Star_Trek)" title="Vulcan (Star Trek)">Vulcans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romulan" title="Romulan">Romulans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tellarite" class="mw-redirect" title="Tellarite">Tellarites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andorian" title="Andorian">Andorians</a>, and Gideonites.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Artist and sculptor <a href="/wiki/Wah_Chang" title="Wah Chang">Wah Chang</a>, who had worked for <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Productions" class="mw-redirect" title="Walt Disney Productions">Walt Disney Productions</a>, was hired to design and manufacture props: he created the flip-open communicator, often credited as having influenced the configuration of the portable version of the <a href="/wiki/Cellular_telephone" class="mw-redirect" title="Cellular telephone">cellular telephone</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WSFTF_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSFTF-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Chang also designed the portable sensing-recording-computing "tricorder" device, and various fictitious devices for the starship's engineering crew and its sick bay. As the series progressed, he helped to create various memorable aliens, such as the <a href="/wiki/Gorn_(Star_Trek)" class="mw-redirect" title="Gorn (Star Trek)">Gorn</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Horta_(Star_Trek)" class="mw-redirect" title="Horta (Star Trek)">Horta</a>.
<span class="anchor" id="S1"></span><span class="anchor" id="Season1"></span><span class="anchor" id="Season_1"></span>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Season_1_(1966–1967)"><span id="Season_1_.281966.E2.80.931967.29"></span>Season 1 (1966–1967)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Season 1 (1966–1967)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1236090951">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series_season_1" title="Star Trek: The Original Series season 1">Star Trek: The Original Series season 1</a></div>
<figure class="mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Shatner_Star_Trek_first_episode_1966.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/William_Shatner_Star_Trek_first_episode_1966.JPG/250px-William_Shatner_Star_Trek_first_episode_1966.JPG" decoding="async" width="180" height="293" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/William_Shatner_Star_Trek_first_episode_1966.JPG/330px-William_Shatner_Star_Trek_first_episode_1966.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/William_Shatner_Star_Trek_first_episode_1966.JPG/500px-William_Shatner_Star_Trek_first_episode_1966.JPG 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="1302" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/William_Shatner" title="William Shatner">William Shatner</a> as <a href="/wiki/James_T._Kirk" title="James T. Kirk">Captain James T. Kirk</a> in action, from the episode "<a href="/wiki/Where_No_Man_Has_Gone_Before" title="Where No Man Has Gone Before">Where No Man Has Gone Before</a>", 1966</figcaption></figure>
<p>NBC ordered 16 episodes of <i>Star Trek</i>, besides "Where No Man Has Gone Before".<sup id="cite_ref-davies2007_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies2007-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The first regular episode of <i>Star Trek</i>, "<a href="/wiki/The_Man_Trap" title="The Man Trap">The Man Trap</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> aired on Thursday, September 8, 1966, from 8:30 to 9:30 as part of an NBC "sneak preview" block. Reviews were mixed; while <i><a href="/wiki/The_Philadelphia_Inquirer" title="The Philadelphia Inquirer">The Philadelphia Inquirer</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Chronicle" title="San Francisco Chronicle">San Francisco Chronicle</a></i> liked the new show, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Boston_Globe" title="The Boston Globe">The Boston Globe</a></i> were less favorable,<sup id="cite_ref-to20060901_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-to20060901-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Variety_(magazine)" title="Variety (magazine)">Variety</a></i> predicted that it "won't work", calling it "an incredible and dreary mess of confusion and complexities".<sup id="cite_ref-millrod19960916_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-millrod19960916-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Debuting against mostly reruns, <i>Star Trek</i> easily won its time slot with a 40.6 share.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The following week against new programming, however, the show fell to second (29.4 share) behind CBS. It ranked 33rd (out of 94 programs) over the next two weeks, then the following two episodes ranked 51st in the ratings.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1224211176">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>I am an avid fan of <i>Star Trek</i>, and would simply die if it was taken off the air. In my opinion it is the best show on television.
</p>
</blockquote>
<div style="padding-bottom: 0; padding-top: 0.5em"><cite class="left-aligned" style="">—M.P., Oswego, New York, February 20, 1967<sup id="cite_ref-tvkey19670220_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tvkey19670220-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></cite></div>
</div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:TOSopeninglogo.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2b/TOSopeninglogo.png/250px-TOSopeninglogo.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/2b/TOSopeninglogo.png 1.5x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="240" /></a><figcaption>Title used for the first season</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Frederik_Pohl" title="Frederik Pohl">Frederik Pohl</a>, editor of <i><a href="/wiki/Galaxy_Science_Fiction" title="Galaxy Science Fiction">Galaxy Science Fiction</a></i>, wrote in February 1967 of his amazement that <i>Star Trek</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s "regular shows were just as good" as the early episodes that won an award at <a href="/wiki/24th_World_Science_Fiction_Convention" title="24th World Science Fiction Convention">Tricon</a> in September. Believing that the show would soon be canceled because of low ratings, he lamented that it "made the mistake of appealing to a comparatively literate group", and urged readers to write letters to help save the show.<sup id="cite_ref-pohl196702_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pohl196702-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Star Trek</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s first-season ratings would in earlier years likely have caused NBC to cancel the show. The network had pioneered research into viewers' <a href="/wiki/Demographic_profile" title="Demographic profile">demographic profiles</a> in the early 1960s, however, and by 1967, it and other networks increasingly considered such data when making decisions;<sup id="cite_ref-pearson2011_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pearson2011-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 115">&#58;&#8202;115&#8202;</span></sup> for example, CBS temporarily canceled <i><a href="/wiki/Gunsmoke" title="Gunsmoke">Gunsmoke</a></i> that year because it had too many older and too few younger viewers.<sup id="cite_ref-to20060901_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-to20060901-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Although Roddenberry later claimed that NBC was unaware of <i>Star Trek</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s favorable demographics,<sup id="cite_ref-thomas19760525_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thomas19760525-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> awareness of <i>Star Trek</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s "quality" audience is what likely caused the network to retain the show after the first and second seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-pearson2011_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pearson2011-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 115">&#58;&#8202;115&#8202;</span></sup> NBC instead decided to order 10 more new episodes for the first season, and order a second season in March 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-davies2007_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies2007-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The network originally announced that the show would air at 7:30–8:30&#160;pm Tuesday, but it was instead given an 8:30–9:30&#160;pm Friday slot when the 1967–68 NBC schedule was released,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> making it less appealing to young adult viewers.<sup id="cite_ref-davies2007_26-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies2007-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:left;" class=""></div>
<p><span class="anchor" id="S2"></span><span class="anchor" id="Season2"></span><span class="anchor" id="Season_2"></span>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Season_2_(1967–1968)"><span id="Season_2_.281967.E2.80.931968.29"></span>Season 2 (1967–1968)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Season 2 (1967–1968)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series_season_2" title="Star Trek: The Original Series season 2">Star Trek: The Original Series season 2</a></div>
<figure class="mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Leonard_Nimoy_William_Shatner_Star_Trek_1968.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Leonard_Nimoy_William_Shatner_Star_Trek_1968.JPG/250px-Leonard_Nimoy_William_Shatner_Star_Trek_1968.JPG" decoding="async" width="180" height="228" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Leonard_Nimoy_William_Shatner_Star_Trek_1968.JPG/330px-Leonard_Nimoy_William_Shatner_Star_Trek_1968.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Leonard_Nimoy_William_Shatner_Star_Trek_1968.JPG/500px-Leonard_Nimoy_William_Shatner_Star_Trek_1968.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1507" data-file-height="1911" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Spock" title="Spock">Spock</a>, Kirk and the <i>Enterprise</i>, 1968</figcaption></figure>
<p><i>Star Trek</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s ratings continued to decline during the second season. Although Shatner expected the show to end after two seasons and began to prepare for other projects,<sup id="cite_ref-lowry19680329_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lowry19680329-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> NBC nonetheless may have never seriously considered canceling the show.<sup id="cite_ref-lowry19680117_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lowry19680117-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-to20060901_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-to20060901-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As early as January 1968, the <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> reported that <i>Star Trek</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s chances for renewal for a third season were "excellent". The show had better ratings for NBC than ABC's competing <i><a href="/wiki/Hondo_(TV_series)" title="Hondo (TV series)">Hondo</a></i>, and the competing CBS programs (number-three <i><a href="/wiki/Gomer_Pyle,_U.S.M.C." class="mw-redirect" title="Gomer Pyle, U.S.M.C.">Gomer Pyle, U.S.M.C.</a></i> and the first half-hour of the number-12 <i>CBS Friday Night Movie</i>) were in the top 15 in the Nielsen ratings.<sup id="cite_ref-lowry19680117_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lowry19680117-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nielsen19671968_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nielsen19671968-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Again, demographics helped <i>Star Trek</i> survive.<sup id="cite_ref-pearson2011_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pearson2011-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 116">&#58;&#8202;116&#8202;</span></sup> Contrary to popular belief among its fans, the show did not have a larger audience of young viewers than its competition while on NBC.<sup id="cite_ref-to20060901_33-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-to20060901-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The network's research did indicate that <i>Star Trek</i> had a "quality audience" including "upper-income, better-educated males", however, and other NBC shows had lower overall ratings.<sup id="cite_ref-pearson2011_40-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pearson2011-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 116">&#58;&#8202;116&#8202;</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lowry19680117_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lowry19680117-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1224211176" /><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>Look! Look! It doesn't stop! They're lined up all the way down the street!
</p>
</blockquote>
<div style="padding-bottom: 0; padding-top: 0.5em"><cite class="left-aligned" style="">—Norman Lunenfeld, NBC executive, on the mail trucks delivering <i>Star Trek</i> fans' letters<sup id="cite_ref-poe1998_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-poe1998-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></cite></div>
</div>
<p>The enthusiasm of <i>Star Trek</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s viewers surprised NBC.<sup id="cite_ref-to20060901_33-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-to20060901-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The show was unusual in its serious discussion of contemporary societal issues in a futuristic context, unlike <i><a href="/wiki/Lost_in_Space" title="Lost in Space">Lost in Space</a></i>, which was more <a href="/wiki/Camp_(style)" title="Camp (style)">campy</a> in nature.<sup id="cite_ref-conley19720416_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-conley19720416-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The network had already received 29,000 fan letters for the show during its first season, more than for any other except <i><a href="/wiki/The_Monkees_(TV_series)" title="The Monkees (TV series)">The Monkees</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-davies2007_26-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies2007-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> When rumors spread in late 1967 that <i>Star Trek</i> was at risk of cancellation, Roddenberry secretly began and funded an effort by <a href="/wiki/Bjo_Trimble" title="Bjo Trimble">Bjo Trimble</a>, her husband John, and other fans to persuade tens of thousands of viewers to write letters of support to save the program.<sup id="cite_ref-poe1998_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-poe1998-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-solow1997_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-solow1997-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 377–394">&#58;&#8202;377–394&#8202;</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-page19680815_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-page19680815-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Using the 4,000 names on a mailing list for a science-fiction convention, the Trimbles asked fans to write to NBC and ask 10 others to also do so.<sup id="cite_ref-michaud1986_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-michaud1986-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 128">&#58;&#8202;128&#8202;</span></sup> NBC received almost 116,000 letters for the show between December 1967 and March 1968, including more than 52,000 in February alone;<sup id="cite_ref-modbee19680414_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modbee19680414-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-davies2007_26-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies2007-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> according to an NBC executive, the network received more than one million pieces of mail but only disclosed the 116,000 figure.<sup id="cite_ref-poe1998_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-poe1998-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Newspaper columnists encouraged readers to write letters to help save what one called "the best science-fiction show on the air".<sup id="cite_ref-scott19680207_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scott19680207-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> More than 200 <a href="/wiki/Caltech" class="mw-redirect" title="Caltech">Caltech</a> students marched to NBC's <a href="/wiki/Burbank,_California" title="Burbank, California">Burbank, California</a> studio to support <i>Star Trek</i> in January 1968, carrying signs such as "<a href="/wiki/Conscription_in_the_United_States" title="Conscription in the United States">Draft</a> Spock" and "<a href="/wiki/Black_Power" class="mw-redirect" title="Black Power">Vulcan Power</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-harrison20110425_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harrison20110425-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">Berkeley</a> and <a href="/wiki/MIT" class="mw-redirect" title="MIT">MIT</a> students organized similar protests in San Francisco and New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-scott19680207_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scott19680207-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The letters supporting <i>Star Trek</i>, whose authors included <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_New_York" title="Governor of New York">New York State Governor</a> <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Rockefeller" title="Nelson Rockefeller">Nelson Rockefeller</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-svetkey19910927_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-svetkey19910927-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> were different in both quantity and quality from most mail that television networks receive:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The show, according to the 6,000 letters it draws a week (more than any other in television), is watched by scientists, museum curators, psychiatrists, doctors, university professors, and other highbrows. The <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a> asked for a print of the show for its archives, the only show so honored.<sup id="cite_ref-scott19680207_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scott19680207-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In addition:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712" /><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Much of the mail came from doctors, scientists, teachers, and other professional people, and was for the most part literate–and written on good stationery. And if there is anything a network wants almost as much as a high Nielsen ratings, it is the prestige of a show that appeals to the upper middle class and high-brow audiences.<sup id="cite_ref-lowry19680329_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lowry19680329-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1224211176" /><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>And now an announcement of interest to all viewers of <i>Star Trek</i>. We are pleased to tell you that <i>Star Trek</i> will continue to be seen on NBC Television. We know you will be looking forward to seeing the weekly adventure in space on <i>Star Trek</i>.
</p>
</blockquote>
<div style="padding-bottom: 0; padding-top: 0.5em"><cite class="left-aligned" style="">—NBC announcer, March 1, 1968<sup id="cite_ref-modbee19680414_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modbee19680414-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-svetkey19910927_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-svetkey19910927-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-to20060901_33-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-to20060901-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></cite></div>
</div>
<p>NBC—which used such anecdotes in much of its publicity for the show—made the unusual decision to announce on television, after the episode "<a href="/wiki/The_Omega_Glory" title="The Omega Glory">The Omega Glory</a>" on March 1, 1968, that the series had been renewed.<sup id="cite_ref-pearson2011_40-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pearson2011-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 116–117">&#58;&#8202;116–117&#8202;</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-svetkey19910927_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-svetkey19910927-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The announcement implied a request to stop writing—NBC's policy of replying to each viewer mail meant that the campaign cost the network millions of dollars<sup id="cite_ref-poe1998_47-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-poe1998-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>—but instead caused fans to send letters of thanks in similar numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
<span class="anchor" id="S3"></span><span class="anchor" id="Season3"></span><span class="anchor" id="Season_3"></span>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Season_3_(1968–1969)"><span id="Season_3_.281968.E2.80.931969.29"></span>Season 3 (1968–1969)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Season 3 (1968–1969)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series_season_3" title="Star Trek: The Original Series season 3">Star Trek: The Original Series season 3</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Leonard_Nimoy_William_Shatner_Spock%27s_Brain_Star_Trek_1968.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Leonard_Nimoy_William_Shatner_Spock%27s_Brain_Star_Trek_1968.JPG/250px-Leonard_Nimoy_William_Shatner_Spock%27s_Brain_Star_Trek_1968.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="192" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Leonard_Nimoy_William_Shatner_Spock%27s_Brain_Star_Trek_1968.JPG/500px-Leonard_Nimoy_William_Shatner_Spock%27s_Brain_Star_Trek_1968.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="932" data-file-height="716" /></a><figcaption>"<a href="/wiki/Spock%27s_Brain" title="Spock&#39;s Brain">Spock's Brain</a>" was the first episode of the third season.</figcaption></figure>
<p>NBC at first planned to move <i>Star Trek</i> to Mondays for the show's third season, likely in hopes of increasing its audience after the enormous letter campaign that surprised the network.<sup id="cite_ref-to20060901_33-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-to20060901-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In March 1968; however, NBC instead moved the show to 10:00&#160;pm <a href="/wiki/Friday_night_death_slot" title="Friday night death slot">Friday night</a>, an hour undesirable for its younger audience,<sup id="cite_ref-page19680815_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-page19680815-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lowry19690521_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lowry19690521-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> so as not to conflict with the highly successful <i><a href="/wiki/Rowan_%26_Martin%27s_Laugh-In" title="Rowan &amp; Martin&#39;s Laugh-In">Rowan &amp; Martin's Laugh-In</a></i> on Monday evenings,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> from whose time slot <i>Laugh-In</i> producer <a href="/wiki/George_Schlatter" title="George Schlatter">George Schlatter</a> had angrily demanded it not be rescheduled. In addition to the undesirable time slot, <i>Star Trek</i> was now being seen on only 181 of NBC's 210 affiliates.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Roddenberry was frustrated, and complained, "If the network wants to kill us, it couldn't make a better move."<sup id="cite_ref-page19680815_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-page19680815-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He attempted to persuade NBC to give <i>Star Trek</i> a better day and hour, but was not successful. As a result of this and his own growing exhaustion, he chose to withdraw from the stress of the daily production of <i>Star Trek</i>, though he remained nominally in charge as its "executive producer".<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Roddenberry reduced his direct involvement in <i>Star Trek</i> before the start of the 1968–69 television season, and was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Fred_Freiberger" title="Fred Freiberger">Fred Freiberger</a> as the producer of the television series. <a href="/wiki/Arthur_H._Singer" title="Arthur H. Singer">Arthur H. Singer</a> served as story editor. NBC next reduced <i>Star Trek's</i> budget from $185,000 per episode in season 2 (it was $190,000 per episode in season 1) to $175,000 per episode in season 3 (as the per-minute commercial price had dropped from $39,000 to $36,000 compared to the season-two time slot).<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This caused what some perceive as a decline in quality for the 1968–69 season, although there was a trade off in some lower production costs since the special effects technology had improved over time. By season 3 William Shatner felt that the main characters had become more compromised or exaggerated and the story lines more improbable.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Leonard Nimoy felt that financial concerns dominated.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Associate Producer Bob Justman, who left during the third season, said budget cuts caused the crew to become necessarily limited in the type of filming that could be done, such as outdoor work,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> with only one episode, "The Paradise Syndrome", shot largely outdoors. Nichelle Nichols described the budget-cutting during the final year as an intentional effort to kill off <i>Star Trek</i>:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712" /><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>While NBC paid lip service to expanding <i>Star Trek</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> audience, it [now] slashed our production budget until it was actually 10% lower than it had been in our first season<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... This is why in the third season you saw fewer outdoor location shots, for example. Top writers, top guest stars, top anything you needed was harder to come by. Thus, <i>Star Trek's</i> demise became a self-fulfilling prophecy. And I can assure you, that is exactly as it was meant to be.<sup id="cite_ref-nichols1994_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nichols1994-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The last day of filming for <i>Star Trek</i> was January 9, 1969,<sup id="cite_ref-davies2007_26-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies2007-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and after 79 episodes<sup id="cite_ref-ST7879_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ST7879-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> NBC canceled the show in February despite fans' attempt at another letter-writing campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-to20060901_33-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-to20060901-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One newspaper columnist advised a protesting viewer:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712" /><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>You <i>Star Trek</i> fans have fought the "good fight", but the show has been cancelled and there's nothing to be done now.<sup id="cite_ref-tvscout19690718_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tvscout19690718-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In 2011, the decision to cancel <i>Star Trek</i> by NBC was ranked number four on the <a href="/wiki/TV_Guide_Network" class="mw-redirect" title="TV Guide Network">TV Guide Network</a> special, <i>25 Biggest TV Blunders 2</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Syndication">Syndication</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Syndication"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1224211176" /><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>Surprisingly, one show no longer programmed by a network but syndicated to local television stations (<i>Star Trek</i>) sometimes appeared among the top-five favorites in areas where the show is carried.
</p>
</blockquote>
<div style="padding-bottom: 0; padding-top: 0.5em"><cite class="left-aligned" style="">—"Students rate television", 1971<sup id="cite_ref-co19710428_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-co19710428-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></cite></div>
</div>
<p>Although some of the third season's episodes were considered of poorer quality, it gave <i>Star Trek</i> enough episodes for <a href="/wiki/Television_syndication" class="mw-redirect" title="Television syndication">television syndication</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-teitelbaum19910505_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-teitelbaum19910505-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Most shows require <a href="/wiki/100_episodes" title="100 episodes">at least four seasons</a> for syndication, because otherwise not enough episodes are available for daily <a href="/wiki/Stripping_(television)" class="mw-redirect" title="Stripping (television)">stripping</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kaiser_Broadcasting" title="Kaiser Broadcasting">Kaiser Broadcasting</a>, however, purchased syndication rights for <i>Star Trek</i> during the first season for its stations in several large cities. The company arranged the unusual deal because it saw the show as effective <a href="/wiki/Counterprogramming_(television)" title="Counterprogramming (television)">counterprogramming</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Big_Three_networks" class="mw-redirect" title="Big Three networks">Big Three networks</a>' 6 pm evening news programs.<sup id="cite_ref-abbott2010_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abbott2010-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 138">&#58;&#8202;138&#8202;</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-davies2007_26-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies2007-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Paramount began advertising the reruns in <a href="/wiki/Trade_press" class="mw-redirect" title="Trade press">trade press</a> in March 1969;<sup id="cite_ref-to20081215_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-to20081215-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> as Kaiser's ratings were good, other stations, such as <a href="/wiki/WPIX" title="WPIX">WPIX</a> in New York City and WKBS in Philadelphia, also purchased the episodes<sup id="cite_ref-meehan2005_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meehan2005-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 91–92">&#58;&#8202;91–92&#8202;</span></sup> for similar counterprogramming.<sup id="cite_ref-pearson2011_40-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pearson2011-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 121">&#58;&#8202;121&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>Through syndication, <i>Star Trek</i> found a larger audience than it had on NBC, becoming a cult classic.<sup id="cite_ref-hale19750426_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hale19750426-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-abbott2010_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abbott2010-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 138–139">&#58;&#8202;138–139&#8202;</span></sup> Airing the show in the late afternoon or early evening attracted many new viewers, often young.<sup id="cite_ref-shult19720703_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shult19720703-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 1970, Paramount's trade advertisements claimed that the show had significantly improved its stations' ratings,<sup id="cite_ref-to20081215_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-to20081215-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a></i> commented on <i>Star Trek</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s ability to "acquire the most enviable ratings in the syndication field".<sup id="cite_ref-pearson2011_40-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pearson2011-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 121">&#58;&#8202;121&#8202;</span></sup> By 1972, what the Associated Press described as "the show that won't die" aired in more than 100 American cities and 60 other countries; and more than 3,000 fans attended the first <a href="/wiki/Star_Trek_convention" class="mw-redirect" title="Star Trek convention"><i>Star Trek</i> convention</a> in New York City in January 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-buck19720314_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buck19720314-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-shult19720703_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shult19720703-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1224211176" /><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>Since that dark day in 1969 when NBC brought the programming hammer down on <i>Star Trek</i>, there probably hasn't been a 24-hour period when the original program, one of the original episodes, wasn't being aired somewhere.
</p>
</blockquote>
<div style="padding-bottom: 0; padding-top: 0.5em"><cite class="left-aligned" style="">—<i>Chicago Tribune</i>, 1987<sup id="cite_ref-daley19870104_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-daley19870104-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></cite></div>
</div>
<p>Fans of the show became increasingly organized, gathering at conventions to trade merchandise, meet actors from the show, and watch screenings of old episodes. Such fans came to be known as "<a href="/wiki/Trekkie" title="Trekkie">trekkies</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-hale19750426_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hale19750426-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> who were noted (and often ridiculed) for their extreme devotion to the show and their encyclopedic knowledge of every episode.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Because fans enjoyed re-watching each episode many times, prices for <i>Star Trek</i> rose over time, instead of falling like other syndicated reruns.<sup id="cite_ref-pearson2011_40-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pearson2011-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 122">&#58;&#8202;122&#8202;</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ei19720506_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ei19720506-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sedgeman19720529_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sedgeman19720529-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hale19750426_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hale19750426-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-montgomery19730311_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-montgomery19730311-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/People_(magazine)" title="People (magazine)">People</a></i> magazine commented in 1977 that the show "threatens to rerun until the universe crawls back into its little black hole".<sup id="cite_ref-darrach19770725_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-darrach19770725-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 1986, 17 years after entering syndication, <i>Star Trek</i> was the most popular syndicated series;<sup id="cite_ref-harmetz19861102_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harmetz19861102-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> by 1987, Paramount made $1&#160;million from each episode;<sup id="cite_ref-davies2007_26-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies2007-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and by 1994, the reruns still aired in 94% of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-cerone19940402_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cerone19940402-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>From September 1 to December 24, 1998, the Sci-Fi Channel broadcast a "Special Edition" of all the original series episodes in an expanded 90-minute format hosted by William Shatner. Now titled <i>Star Trek: The Original Series</i>, these broadcasts restored scenes that had been edited out of the syndicated episodes.<sup id="cite_ref-tos_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tos-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In addition to introductory and post-episode commentary by Shatner, the episodes included interviews with members of the regular production team and cast, writers, guest stars, and critics (titled as "<i>Star Trek</i> Insights"). The episodes were broadcast in the original broadcast sequence, followed by "The Cage", to which a full 105-minute segment was devoted. (For details on each episode's original airdate, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series_episodes" title="List of Star Trek: The Original Series episodes">List of <i>Star Trek: The Original Series</i> episodes</a>.) Leonard Nimoy hosted a second run from December 28, 1998, to March 24, 1999, but not all the episodes were broadcast because the show was abruptly canceled before completion.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (January 2012)">original research?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Remastered_edition">Remastered edition</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Remastered edition"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>To celebrate the series' 40th anniversary in September 2006, CBS Paramount Domestic Television (now known as <a href="/wiki/CBS_Media_Ventures" title="CBS Media Ventures">CBS Media Ventures</a>, the current rights holders for the <i>Star Trek</i> television franchises) began syndication of an enhanced version of <i>Star Trek: The Original Series</i> in <a href="/wiki/High-definition_television" title="High-definition television">high definition</a> with new <a href="/wiki/Computer-generated_imagery" title="Computer-generated imagery">CGI</a> visual effects.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Under the direction of <i>Star Trek</i> producer David Rossi, who consulted with Mike and Denise Okuda, the visual and special effects were recreated to give <i>Star Trek: The Original Series</i> a more modern look. Special attention was given to such elements as the <i>Enterprise</i>, alien planets and their images depicted from space, planets seen from orbit, alien spacecraft, and technology such as computer readouts, viewscreen images, and phaser beams.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The restoration and enhancement was performed by <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS Digital</a>. All live-action footage was scanned in high definition from its first-generation 35&#160;mm film elements. While it was possible to retouch and remaster some visual effects, all new exterior ship, space, and planet shots were recreated under the supervision of Niel Wray.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>"Original camera negatives" were used for all live-action footage, but not for external shots of the ship and planets. Notable changes include new space shots with a CGI <i>Enterprise</i>, and other new models (for example, a <a href="/wiki/Gorn_(Star_Trek)" class="mw-redirect" title="Gorn (Star Trek)">Gorn</a> ship is shown in "<a href="/wiki/Arena_(Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series)" title="Arena (Star Trek: The Original Series)">Arena</a>"), redone <a href="/wiki/Matte_(filmmaking)" title="Matte (filmmaking)">matte</a> background shots, and other minor touches such as tidying up viewscreens.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>A small number of scenes were also recomposed, and sometimes new actors were placed into the background of shots.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The opening theme music was also re-recorded in digital stereo.
</p><p>The first episode to be released to syndication was "<a href="/wiki/Balance_of_Terror" title="Balance of Terror">Balance of Terror</a>" on the weekend of September 16, 2006. Episodes were released at the rate of about one a week and broadcast in a 4:3 aspect ratio. Despite the <a href="/wiki/High-definition_video" title="High-definition video">HD</a> remastering, CBS delivered the broadcast syndication package in <a href="/wiki/Standard-definition_television" title="Standard-definition television">Standard Definition (SD TV)</a>. The HD format was made commercially available through <a href="/wiki/Blu-ray_Disc" class="mw-redirect" title="Blu-ray Disc">Blu-ray</a>, or by streaming and downloads.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>While the CGI shots were mastered in a 16:9 aspect ratio for future applications, they were initially broadcast in the U.S. and Canada—along with the live-action footage—in a 4:3 aspect ratio to respect the show's original composition.
</p><p>On July 26, 2007, <a href="/wiki/CBS_Home_Entertainment" title="CBS Home Entertainment">CBS Home Entertainment</a> (with distribution by Paramount Home Entertainment) announced that the remastered episodes of <i>TOS</i> would be released on an <a href="/wiki/HD_DVD" title="HD DVD">HD DVD</a>/DVD hybrid format. Season one was released on November 20, 2007. Season two had been scheduled for release in the summer of 2008, but it was canceled when <a href="/wiki/Toshiba" title="Toshiba">Toshiba</a> (which had been helping finance the remastering of the show) pulled out of the HD DVD business.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On August 5, 2008, the remastered season two was released on DVD only.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Season 3 was released on DVD on November 18, 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On February 17, 2009, Paramount announced the season one of <i>TOS</i> on <a href="/wiki/Blu-ray_Disc" class="mw-redirect" title="Blu-ray Disc">Blu-ray Disc</a> for a May release to coincide with the new feature film coming from Paramount.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The second season was released in a seven disc set on Blu-ray in the U.S. on September 22, 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The third season was released on Blu-ray in the U.S. on December 15.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With the
